[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews 'Ssh! We have a Plan', an environmental treasure for all ages.\xa9 2016

<img alt="Book Cover: Shh! We have a plan, by Chris Haughton" src="https://41.media.tumblr.com/831e886b5a3772c6daf5f5e3b5ad79f3/tumblr_nkv1gnSwi01qa75lmo1_500.jpg"/><strong>\u201c<em>Peace cannot be kept by force: it can only be achieved by understanding</em>\u201d Albert Einstein.</strong><em>Shh! We have a Plan</em> is a reminder yet again of the incredible power that story books hold.  Pages that provide a place where both visual understanding and auditory comprehension come together to stretch the mind and push the limits of the reader\u2019s imagination.Filled with roughly torn, collaged shapes in a tonal scheme of blue, purple and white, readers are introduced to a world that looks rather bleak at first, and feels cold and lonely. Four young hunters are out in the dark fields with backpacks and nets and when they see a beautifully vibrant bird, the reader realizes that they also carry with them, a cage.  The game is on as the youngsters try desperately to catch this vivid creature in a bid to harness it\u2019s glory.However, one teammate plays the game of 'Catch' in a new way and approaches the bird in an entirely different manner, which creates a knock-on effect that has the hunters running in fear\u2026.<strong>As children worldwide face Global Warming as the biggest threat of our time, this book\u2019s gentle and peaceful introduction to the world is simply magnificent.</strong>Through the story children are made aware of the need to \u2018capture\u2019 what appeals to mankind, to keep the beauty for ourselves.  Then it shows them how to stop.  It shows them how to see.  And then, how to value all that lies around them.The build-up in the illustrations is just as powerful. As hunter Number Four changes the game, the play on complimentary colours against the monochrome blues heightens the effect on reader\u2019s eyes, until the illustrations are almost so bright that readers have no choice, but to turn the page.<img alt="Illustration: Boy with Birds, Shh! We Have A Plan." src="http://100scopenotes.com/files/2014/10/Shh-We-Have-a-Plan-1-500x260.jpg"/><strong>The effect is startling.</strong>A thousand, brightly rendered birds become rather overwhelming, the colours enhancing the emotional effect on viewers as the characters react. The imagery is wonderfully artistic and quite forcefully striking, while the story itself, on the other hand, is a quiet and peaceful read in contrast, leaving the action to happen inside the reader's imagination.In order to protect the environment, children need to first respect their world and to find value in it's beauty.  But in order to value the freedom of beauty, it must first be captured, enclosed and contained.<em>Shh! We have a Plan</em> does this, enhancing the notion of being boxed in as <strong>Chris Haughton\u2019s</strong> blocks of colour demarcate the world into solid shapes, losing the essence and magic of nature\u2019s life-force.  And there, in these wonderful pages, is a delightful rendition of dystopia, a story begging for human rhyme and reason, as children colour in computer screens with their fingers, and forget that there is a world dying around them.This is a meaningful story that truly deserves to be shared, owned and discussed, for <em>Shh! We have a Plan</em> carries the message of our children's future. <strong><em>Winner of the 2014 Association of Illustrators' Award for Children's Books.</em></strong><strong>See video here:</strong> https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uyJx9cu7snc<img alt="Logo: AOI Awards" src="http://www.theaoi.com/images/awards_logo.png"/>                2/8/2016 10:41:11 AM\r                            2/8/2016 10:46:13 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">My Ouma is 'n Film-ster\xa9 2016

<strong>Jaco Jacobs</strong> weet beslis hoe om \u2018n situasie te skep wat elkeen van ons net kan droom om in te wees, een wat ander jou beny en waaruit jy as \u2018n wenner en \u2018n held  kan stap.<br>Dis so \u2018n situasie waarin Rikus hom bevind. Met \u2018n ouma wat \u2018n bekende rock-ster is, is daar nou wel nie baie tyd om saam met haar te kuier nie, maar daar is ander bonusse: soos om haar nuutste CDs en plakkate te kry.<br> En die heel beste nog is wanneer sy vir hom en sy beste pel, Puleng, rolle as ekstras kry toe sy \u2018n filmrol voor die bekende aktrise, Penny Pinkerton, se neus wegraap! En wanneer hulle met \u2018n helikopter van die huis af filmstel toe vervoer word en uitvind hulle gaan teenoor die bekende tweeling, Lindie en Cindy Adams optree, kan hulle uit hulle vel spring van opwinding. Wag tot hulle maats en familie hiervan hoor!<br>Maar sake op stel verloop van \u2018n vervelige gewag om geroep te word tot \u2018n frantiese geskarrel om Rikus uit \u2018n gevaarlike booswig se kloue te kry en Ouma se filmloopbaan te red.<br><em>My Ouma is \u2018n Film-ster</em> is \u2018n lekkerlees- storie propvol humor, interessante karakters en spanning wat jou op die punt van jou stoel gaan hou. Lesers word agter die skerms van \u2018n filmstel geneem waar \u201caksie\u201d \u2018n nuwe betekenis kry! Gaan ons twee maats vinnig genoeg kan dink en optree om die skurk se planne deurmekaar te krap?<br>Nie net skep Jaco Jacobs wonderlike verhoudings tussen Rikus, sy ouers, sy ouma en Puleng nie, hy wys ook dat jy nie mense in kategorie\xeb moet inperk  net omdat hulle \u2018n sekere ouderdom, geslag of ras is nie. Party oumas brei, bak en vul blokraaisels in \u2013 en daar\u2019s absoluut niks mee verkeerd nie. Maar ander oumas het groen hare, rits in \u2018n rooi sportmotor rond en skuur skouers met beroemdes en staatspresidente - en daar's ook niks daarmee verkeerd nie!<br>Die  lekker verteltrant,  groot teksfont en Stephen Wallace se illustrasies wat rock maak dit maklik om jou in \u2018n japtrap in die verhaal te verloor en dan gaan jy vir seker nie die boek kan neersit voor jy die laaste woord verslind het nie.Lekker lees!</br></br></br></br></br></br>ResensieLiby Nel                1/28/2016 11:32:20 AM\r                            1/28/2016 11:32:20 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews 'The Thing About Jellyfish' by Ali Benjamin \xa9 2016

<img alt="Book Cover: The Thing about Jellyfish by Ali Benjamin" src="http://www.hachettebookgroup.com/_b2c/media/cache/ea/25/ea25246394c1503105017cec3fce0416.jpg"/>Mrs Turton, Suzy\u2019s Grade 7 Life Science teacher, has set the class a science paper, so Suzy Swanson chooses to do her research on Global Warming and the Irukandji jellyfish, a creature whose venom is incredibly potent. Jellyfish are strange, but beautiful. They don\u2019t have hearts, yet they pulse like hearts as they swim through the ocean.  And ever since Mum said the words \u201c<em>Franny Jackson drowned</em>\u201d, Suzy has had to come to terms with the fact that her friend\u2019s heart has stopped beating and it will never beat again.Suzy met Franny at swimming lessons when they were five, but something really horrible happened between them before she died. This means that the grieving process will be a difficult one for Suzy as she silently wades through the dark waters of pain and despair to find healing.<em>The thing about Jellyfish</em> is a fascinating story that takes young readers into the depths of unspoken grief, providing a hard look at a character who is unable to separate herself from the trauma of loss.<strong>Intense and deeply emotive, it\u2019s easy to disappear into this story</strong>.  <strong>Ali Benjamin</strong> pays close attention to the emotional state of Suzy\u2019s mind, leading the reader directly to the core of Suzy\u2019s psyche, allowing them to be sucked into the pain, the confusion, and the questioning.  Written in the first person narrative, the all-consuming loss is quite overwhelming, but at the same time immensely powerful.<strong>Benjamin</strong> has Suzy speaking and thinking in what the family call \u2018constant talk\u2019, as she stores facts like an encyclopedia - her words introspective, flowing chaotically and haphazardly and sometimes skipping specific details as she glosses over ideas or leaves them behind altogether. It is a wonderful process of information, discovery and healing, the story line following the class science project and joining the puzzle pieces together for young readers.Suzy\u2019s head is filled with the biology of animals and plant matter, environmental phenomenon, the ocean\u2019s garbage tides, carbon footprints and global warming making this novel a truly green read. Written in such a way as to be wholly absorbing, readers will find themselves repeating the scientific facts and empathizing, not only with Suzy, but with the planet as a whole.<img alt="Photo: Jellyfish, Goodreads.com" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447345235i/16990659.jpg"/>Catching hints of sunlight, the iridescent green cover shows a young girl holding the tendril of a jellyfish as if she holds onto her dreams as they float above her.  Like a balloon, the creature symbolizes the hope of childhood, while implying too, the desperate threat of danger.  Dreams are scary and strange \u2013 and jellyfish are too - and sometimes children are too young to realise that they are not responsible for what happens in life.This is a story about swimmers and children and friendships, but while the journey is about grief, the moral of this tale is concerned with the living of life and the love that accompanies it.  The ending was exactly as I\u2019d have wanted it and children of all ages will not be disappointed.  They will also know everything there is to know, about jellyfish!<strong>Watch the book trailer <a href="http://www.hachettebookgroup.com/titles/ali-benjamin/the-thing-about-jellyfish/9780316380867/">here.</strong>                3/3/2016 2:03:45 PM\r                            3/4/2016 5:50:53 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews popular international dystopian novel.\xa9 2016

<img alt="Book Cover: The White Rose by Amy Ewing" src="http://ecx.images-amazon.com/images/I/515TRmrtXBL._SX324_BO1,204,203,200_.jpg"/><strong>This Young Adult Fantasy novel packs a real punch from the first chapter, putting the reader directly into the action of the story.</strong><em>The White Rose</em> is the second book in the <strong>\u2018A Lone City\u2019 trilogy</strong>, after debut novel <em>The Jewel</em>; a continuation of <strong>Amy Ewing\u2019s</strong> dystopian world where people are no longer considered human, but are treated as merchandise and test subjects.Thrust into a whirlwind of various characters and their emotional power battles, secret trysts, vicious attacks and revenge, alongside the fears of forbidden love, readers will soon discover that <em>The White Rose</em> has a hard-hitting and rather violent introduction. She must watch as her friends are tortured and killed in an effort to get her to uncover her relationship and to discover why she is not pregnant already. Now that Violet has fallen in love, she has sacrificed everything and her only hope of surviving is to escape. Violet must find the White Rose in order to change the city forever. Secret friends and an ancient underground society, the Black Key, come to Violet\u2019s rescue and through suspense and mystery, sinister hatred, secret passages and great excitement, readers are introduced to the exciting new world of the Lone City.The city itself is well explained and the environment different enough that each chapter is more exciting than the last. Filled with strange intuitive powers and new experiences, <em>The White Rose</em> will keep the adrenaline flowing and hold readers to the end.<strong>A frenetic read from the very first page</strong>A few times there is the need to stop reading and catch one's breath. Some scenes are really uncomfortable - dark, bizarre and disturbing - but teenage readers who enjoyed the Hunger Games will enjoy the similarities of this story.<em>The White Rose</em> has a compelling ecological message within it and the plot highlights humanity\u2019s need for freedom against oppression. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Review 1: 'Mother to Mother' by Dr. Sindiwe Magona.\xa9 2016

<img alt="Book Cover: Mother to Mother by Sindiwe Magona" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/books/1320421141l/85677.jpg"/><strong>At the Puku Story Festival in Grahamstown, February 2016, Dr. Sindiwe Magona, acclaimed author and South African storyteller, was highlighted for her contribution to children as well as for the vast amount of work she has produced over the years.</strong> Along with the launch of her latest book '<em>Chasing The Tails of my Father's Cattle</em>', Puku was also proud to host the stage production of '<em>Mother to Mother</em>', the story Dr Magona began to write when she discovered that Fulbright Scholar, Amy Biehl, had been killed.<em>Mother to Mother</em>, a compelling story of forgiveness and reconciliation, was then adapted for the stage in collaboration with director, Janice Honeyman and powerhouse actress, Thembi Mtshali, translated and shown at the Puku Story Festival, 2016. For many of the visiting isiXhosa-speaking children, this was their first theatre production.Puku thanks the following international writers who volunteered their time to review the book <em>Mother to Mother</em> for Puku: <strong>Robyn Ryle, Caroline Tanski, Amanda Hart, Katrina Otuonye and Ian Riggins.</strong>Their reviews will follow in the week.<hr/><h2>Review #1 by Robyn Ryle</h2>Avoiding complexity in storytelling isn\u2019t hard. It\u2019s easy to fall into the undemanding trope of black and white, villain and victim, or wrong and right. But there\u2019s a price to pay for giving in to the lure of simplicity. As James Baldwin said in <em>Notes of a Native Son</em>, \u201c<em>only within this web of ambiguity, paradox, this hunger, danger, darkness, can we find at once ourselves and the power that will free us from ourselves</em>.\u201d <strong>It takes courage to confront real stories in all their ambiguity, paradox and darkness.</strong> <strong>Sindiwe Magona</strong> demonstrates just that courage in her novel, <em>Mother to Mother</em>, set in the complicated world of Capetown, South Africa in the last days of Apartheid.The inspiration for the novel begins with the real-life murder of Amy Biehl, a white, American, Fulbright Scholar in South Africa working to help organize the nation\u2019s first ever democratic elections. While giving several of her friends a ride home to Guguletu, a township in Capetown, Biehl was dragged out of her car and killed. One of the young men held responsible for Biehl\u2019s death lived next door to Magona. In <em>Mother to Mother</em>, Magona imagines a letter written by Mandisa, the mother of the murderer, to the mother of the victim.<em>Mother to Mother</em> is Mandisa\u2019s story. We flash back to her own childhood and her family\u2019s traumatic forced removal at the hands of the South African government from Blouvlei to Gugulethu. As a young boy, Mxolisi, Mandisa\u2019s son and the killer, accidentally gives away the location of two neighbor boys and witnesses them being shot dead by the police. He doesn\u2019t speak for years afterward. In flashback, Mandisa sifts through the material of her and her son\u2019s life, searching for a single event that explains the coming tragedy.In the present tense of the novel, we follow Mandisa through a day that starts out normal and turns tragic. Through her, we are given a window into life in Gugulethu township in the waning days of Apartheid. We watch as she leaves her children at home unsupervised to clean house for a white Afrikaaner. We see the contrast between her own life and that of Mrs. Nelson, her employee. Magona conveys the generation gap between Mandisa and her children, who like many young people in Gugulethu, are no longer attending school. They\u2019ve responded to calls from the Congress of South African Students to stay away from schools in support of the teachers\u2019 strikes. Mandisa suspects that they may also be participating in a more violent form of protest, burning cars and driving reactionary elements out of the township. Over the course of two days, we follow Mandisa forward to the dawning revelation that her worst fears about her son have come true.Mandisa reveals her and her son\u2019s story, not to excuse his behaviors, but to construct for herself a narrative that makes sense of a tragedy much larger than her and her family. She cannot resist the lure of the what-if. <em>What if the white woman would have realized how dangerous it was to drive through Gugulethu on that day?</em> <em>Why didn\u2019t the young woman feel a natural sense of unease?</em> <em>What if she were not blinded from the danger of what she was doing by her sense of its rightness?</em> The questions echo Mandisa\u2019s own concerns about Mxolisi. Her story is not a justification, but an attempt to understand the vast distance between her son and the young woman he killed, as well as the ways in which they are so very alike.<img alt="Photo: Robyn Ryle" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1420550844p5/4966842.jpg"/><em>Mother to Mother</em> is an important novel for the detailed and well-drawn insight it provides into a particular historical moment. More than that, Magona\u2019s novel is worthwhile for the bravery with which she confronts the hunger, danger and darkness that come with facing humanity in all its tortured complexity.<hr/><strong><em>Robyn Ryle teaches sociology to college students when she\u2019s not writing.</em></strong> <strong><em>Her short story chapbook, The Face of Baseball, is available from WhiskeyPaper Press. She has stories and essays in CALYX Journal, Midwestern Gothic, Big Truths/Little Fiction and Bartleby Snopes, among others.</em></strong> <strong><em>You can find her on Twitter,</em></strong> <a href="https://twitter.com/robynryle">@RobynRyle.                5/16/2016 10:09:36 AM\r                            5/18/2016 10:45:45 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">PUKU resenseer Ek en Prins Donkerkuif deur Dihanna Taute\xa9 2016

Lehandi Botha is glad nie be\u0457ndruk met hulle trekkery Pretoria toe nie. Dis vir haar so duidelik soos daglig dat hulle soos in glad nie hier gaan inpas nie. En jy sal nie spyt wees as jy dit doen nie.<br/>Ek en Prins Donkerkuif is nou op die rakke by boekhandelaars soos die CNA en Exclusive Books. Gaan koop joune sommer dadelik.<br/>                5/23/2016 4:33:35 PM\r                            5/23/2016 4:33:35 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews 'Underwater' by Marisa Reichardt\xa9 2016

<img alt="Book Cover: Underwater by Marisa Reichardt" src="http://www.panmacmillan.co.in/Images/books/SmallImage/9781509818006.jpg"/>The inviting image of a swimmer quietly floating in sparkling water is <em>Underwater's</em> front cover image; a place of peace and tranquility.  The story, however, flows into the depths of the psyche, into the dark chasms of the mind where fear and terror hide in the shadows.Included in Morgan\u2019s experiences, the reader is introduced to a teenager who obviously loves her family, studies online from home, stays inside her flat all day and goes no further than her own front door.  Morgan\u2019s viewpoint stops there, you see. Gradually the story reveals that she\u2019s not well.  Consumed by fear, Morgan has experienced a trauma of some sort, something so big that she is incapable of living a normal life.Until Evan moves in next door. Cute, friendly and of a similar age, Evan seems genuinely interested in Morgan and her family.  He's vibrant, energetic and everything about him reminds Morgan of the sun and sea \u2013 sensations that she has forgotten and longs to return to.  Between Evan\u2019s visits and those of the weekly therapist, Brenda, the plot is all about Morgan\u2019s journey to recovery, a mad swim through the memories that terrorize her reality and hold her captive. Evan has more in common with Morgan than she realises, but the links can only be discovered as each detail of the past is unwrapped, allowing Morgan to talk about the boy who came to school loaded with guns\u2026<strong><em>Underwater</em> is a brilliant read, easy to follow, completely absorbing and incredibly addictive.</strong><img alt="Photo: Underwater by Hashtaglovebooks" src="http://i0.wp.com/hashtaglovebooks.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/04/r3ji2pe.png?resize=472,408"/>Through an intense first person narrative, <strong>Marisa Reichardt</strong> builds up the pressure, leading the reader step by step into the abyss.  Finely tuned hooks, all pieces of the puzzle, keep the reader ensnared despite the need to stop for air. There is a feeling of heaviness and suffocation, and the subject matter may well put readers off, but there is enough love and compassion to balance a very well written story.<strong>Young readers are guaranteed a roller coaster ride.</strong>As violence in South Africa escalates, with more and more children living in fear, <em>Underwater</em> is a wonderfully gentle and sensitive voice in the darkness.  Many readers will relate to Morgan's fears and most will benefit from this story. After all, while a situation takes only seven minutes to happen, the effects can last for years.For Morgan, Evan is her refuge, an anchor in the storm and a truly delightful promise of hope to look forward to. <em>Underwater</em> is told with deep understanding and empathy - sometimes emotional, other times heavy \u2013 but generally stirring and thought-provoking. The story is relevant and deals with important questions, but most importantly, readers are forced to be a part of the process and are encouraged to learn from it.  <strong>This is a story that cannot be missed.</strong><strong>Watch the book trailer <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hRJTGT3F4Aw">here.</strong><img alt="Logo: Pan Macmillan" src="http://www.warwickcairns.com/Site/About_Me_files/macmillan_logo.jpg"/>                5/24/2016 9:44:20 AM\r                            5/26/2016 10:18:39 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews 'Angry Owl' by Kerryn Ponter\xa9 2016

<strong><em>Angry Owl</em> is a proudly South African picture book, written and illustrated by local author Kerryn Ponter.</strong> <strong>It is the story of\u2026 well, an angry owl, but the question is \u2018Why is he so angry?\u2019</strong><img alt="Book Cover: Angry Owl by Kerryn Ponter, RSA" src="http://www.randomstruik.co.za/bookTitles/sml/9781432306076.jpg"/>Poor Owl, it seems, sits outside in all sorts of ghastly weather, facing rain, storms, blizzards, lightening, hail and snow, which leave him bedraggled and looking worse for wear.\u201c<em>He looks like a dirty cleaning rag</em>,\u201d said one Puku reviewer.The story line is wonderfully simple and filled with useful adjectives that will teach kids how to properly describe the weather.  The illustrations enhance the story and highlight Owl\u2019s dilemma, while at the same time keeping him amusing and light-hearted, (for owls after all, can handle any weather.) The storm itself, has a beautiful ending that will satisfy children and have them returning for more.For very young readers, the lessons lie in the weather patterns, introducing them to the force of nature upon the environment and the feelings that weather evokes. <strong>But older children, too, will benefit from Angry Owl.</strong>One twelve-year old picked up this A4 softcover, looked askance at the cover and muttered\u201c<em>How does the cover relate to the title? The owl doesn\u2019t look very angry\u2026</em>\u201d  Encouraged to open the book, he read the story out loud and when he was finished, exclaimed \u201c<em>That was awesome!</em>\u201d\u201c<em>It\u2019s like really related to life.  If you\u2019re going through a tough time and it\u2019s really bad, you know that something good will come out at the end.  I like this book.  The illustrations were cool and I enjoyed the details that were added to the owl, and to the seasonal weather, and all the extras that came with the storm as it changed</em>.\u201d\u201c<em>In the end</em>,\u201d he continued, \u201c<em>the cover did make sense, because Angry Owl was only angry in horrible weather and I would be feeling the same in that situation</em>.\u201d<img alt="Illustration: Angry Owl by Kerryn Ponter" src="http://www.jozikids.co.za/uploadimages/untitled_9868_tiny.png"/>\u201c<em>I didn\u2019t understand at first why he was holding an umbrella on the cover, but once you read the book you get why there is an umbrella in his hand \u2013 because he sits outside all the time in such awful weather! There was even some extra fun in thinking of different adjectives, wording and colloquialisms to go with the story to make it more age-appropriate.In the end <em>Angry Owl\u2019s</em> mind is right and his expressions have changed. Even the little birds around him are happy and are filled with bright spring colours.  <em>Angry Owl</em> can\u2019t stop the weather, so he has to learn to accept it.  He knows being angry doesn\u2019t help - and that\u2019s a good lesson for any child to learn, no matter what age they are.<img alt="Logo: Struik Kinders" src="http://www.randomstruik.co.za/images/struik-kinders/logo.gif"/>Puku readers have a passion for environmental stories and this story is a very good place to start.<strong>Ook in Afrikaans beskikbaar as <a href="http://www.randomstruik.co.za/books/kwaai-uil/6122">Kwaai Uil</strong>                6/21/2016 9:02:41 AM\r                            6/21/2016 9:25:27 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">GUNSTELINGVLEGSELS \xa9 2016

Met <em>Gunstelingvlegsels</em> is \u201cbad-hair days\u201d iets van die verlede. Sy en <strong>Marie</strong> het op mekaar se hare geoefen en heeltemal verlief geraak op vleg \u2013 met onweerstaanbare resultate.<br/><em>Gunstelingvlegsels</em> bied maklike tot gevorderde vlegselstyle. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews 'The Hired Girl' by Laura Amy Schlitz\xa9 2016

<img alt="enter image description here" src="http://www.candlewick.com/images/cwp_bookjackets/648/076367818X.jpg"/><strong>The year is 1911 and Joan Skraggs has had to leave school to carry out her duties on the family farm. Her mother has passed away and her father believes that educating Joan is a waste of time.</strong><em>The Hired Girl</em>, likened to <em>Jane Eyre</em> and with the same tones of <em>Downtown Abbey</em>, is the story of a fourteen-year-old girl\u2019s journey into womanhood and emancipation.  Writing in order to continue her knowledge of language, Joan\u2019s diary highlights the suppression of women at a time when schooling was primarily for men.  When her books are burnt as a punishment, she runs away and catches a train to Baltimore. It is here that she lands a job as <em>The Hired Girl</em> with a high-society Jewish family, and where they in return are so inspired by Joan\u2019s love for learning, that they take her into their hearts.<strong>\u201c<em>I\u2019ve missed the feeling of owning a book.  Not having any books makes me feel empty and strained and pathetic.</em>\u201d</strong>Her new environment introduces cultural and religious experiences, that make Joan question her Presbyterian upbringing and the values of her own traditions. Written in diary format in the first person narrative, each entry is a surprise that is wonderfully fresh and vibrant, allowing the reader to be a part of Joan\u2019s adventures. In an African context this is a tale that is as familiar as it is foreign, where rural settings vie against cityscapes and cultures clash across money and education. South African readers will enjoy Joan\u2019s passion for language, as well as  her avid love for books and some may even take a step further to research the classics that this character so enjoys.<strong>Schlitz\u2019s writing is beautiful and her expressions simply delicious:</strong>\u201c<em>My hatred has crawled into the pillow slip and made a lump</em>.\u201dThe author creates a tangible space, not only for the story, but for each written word. The characters are reminiscent of those so well observed in tales like <em>How to kill a Mockingbird</em> and <em>Little Women</em>, yet sadly, Joan\u2019s yearning to become a writer is unresolved towards the end and the joy of language itself dissipates, leaving the reader feeling that the ending is somewhat rushed and inconclusive.The front cover of the book is rendered like a Constable portrait and seems rather drab next to the vibrant African covers that grace modern bookshelves at present. This may put buyers off initially, but by the end of the book the cover makes more sense.<img alt="Logo: Walker Books Ltd" src="http://martynbedford.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Walker-Books-logo.png"/>Inevitably freedom shines through, marking the rights of children to education and embracing the joy of learning. Teachers will find this novel a useful tool in the classroom in discussions around social issues - religious persecution, cultural differences and societal oppression - as young readers observe Joan breaking the rules, escaping her confines and searching for liberty.<strong>Joan is brave and courageous - readers will want to applaud her, and that is a wonderful feeling to find in any book.</strong><strong>Video Trailer:</strong> <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=J2AbHL2r-7w">Hear more from the author here.                7/19/2016 8:49:56 AM\r                            7/20/2016 7:08:51 AM\r

Die leser word betrek en voel bemagtig deur fisiese deelname aan \u2018n aktiwiteit om al die verdwene partye terug te kry uit die boek uit. Bella gee opdrag, met duidelike illustrasies, dat die boek op sy sy gedraai moet word en dan geskud en geskud en geskud moet word. Met die laaste prettige \u2018skud\u2019, verskyn al die karakters weer op die bladsye en alles is normaal \u2013 wel amper, want, wanneer Bella se hond verskyn lyk hy \u2018n bietjie anders.<br/>\u2018n Kostelike illustrasie toon Bella se hond met sy agterpootjies  op sy rug geteken en sy stertjie in die plek van die pootjies! Di\xe9 skreeusnaakse tekening sorg vir baie pret en stel die jong leser terselfdertyd gerus dat enige iemand \u2018n fout kan maak, selfs met \u2018n skets in \u2018n storieboek.                 9/12/2016 3:13:27 PM\r                            9/12/2016 3:13:27 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Die Engelsman in my slaai\xa9 2016

Vir diegene wat <strong>Dihanna Taute</strong> se debuutroman, <em>Ek en Prins Donkerkuif</em> geniet het, het ons goeie nuus. Die tweede boek in haar trilogie, <em>Die Engelsman in my Slaai</em> is nou op die winkelrakke.<br/>In <em>Die Engelsman in my Slaai</em> ontmoet ons \u2018n meer selfversekerde Lehandi en dit lyk of sy elke greintjie selfvertroue gaan nodig h\xea om die nuwe uitdagings in haar lewe te hanteer. Haar pa en sy flooze verwag \u2018n nuwe baba en haar ma begin uitgaan met die pa van haar aartsvyand by die skool! Duh...regtig?! <br/>En dan is daar die GROOT probleem met die see-groen o\xeb en die glimlag wat jou knie\xeb in jellie verander. Dit maak nie saak hoe hard Lehandi met haar vriendinne hieroor stry of vir haarself vertel sy en Drake is nog steeds \u2018n item nie, diep in haar hart weet sy die Engelsman is mega-moeilikheid.<br/>Hy zoem onbeskaamd op haar in en sy weet hy\u2019s player! Help!<br/>Maar dinge werk nie altyd uit soos \u2018n mens dink dit gaan nie.<br/><em>Die Engelsman in my Slaai</em> is \u2018n heerlike leesboek vol humor, spanning en romanse wat veral deur tienermeisies geniet sal word omdat hulle met alles daarin sal kan identifiseer. <strong>Taute</strong> ken nou maar eenmaal die w\xeareld en die hart van \u2018n jongmens en sy lei ons katvoet deur al die strikke waarin \u2018n tiener kan trap.<br/>Met \u2018n paar goedgekose woorde skep sy \u2018n groep interessante en uiteenlopende karakters wat kontrasteer, mekaar verkeerd opvryf of mekaar aanvul.<br/>Te midde van al die pret, flankeerdery en ademlose oomblikke skemer die ander kant van die muntstuk elke nou en dan deur om \u2018n kykie te bied in die gedagtes agter Lehandi se glimlag. Die onsekerheid oor ouers wat nuwe lewenspaaie aanpak - en waar los dit haar?; die hartseer van jong liefde wat vervaag; die ontdekking van jou eie identiteit en die terugslaan van balle wat die lewe onverwags jou kant toe gooi.<br/><em>Die Engelsman in my Slaai</em> laat die leser met 'n lekker gevoel om die hart en 'n "kan nie wag nie" uitsien na die laaste boek in die trilogie, <em>\u2018n Blonde Bom</em>, waarin Lehandi voor haar grootste uitdagings nog te staan kom.                9/12/2016 3:09:02 PM\r                            9/12/2016 3:09:02 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Jay Heale Reviews The Land of Kachoo series\xa9 2016

Three African animal tales told in verse text, with lively illustrations. In fact, this is Visual Marketing: amusing colour pictures + a page of Stickers (relevant to each story). The verse is mostly lame with a few ghastly couplets such as: \u201cMaybe a hippo I could mistakenly be / But my black and white coat will a predator see\u201d \u2013 that\u2019s horribly forced. Nor do I understand \u201camong the hippopotami bloat\u201d (to rhyme with \u201cfloat\u201d). Bloated hippopotami perhaps? The setting is picture-postcard African veld, though there is no consistency. THE LION HUNTS suddenly introduces a mongoose living behind a door with furniture and electric light. LOST is a straightforward small creature (zebra) looking for its mummy. Vaguely enjoyable. WHO IS KING decides that Lion is easily the most \u201cbrave and tall, filled with pride\u201d. But THE LION HUNTS portrays Lion as unable to hunt very well and \u201cfeeling quite mean\u201d. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews Oh, What a Tangle by Anita Pouroulis\xa9 2016

Oh, What a Tangle! is an intriguing story about the adventures of Kiki\u2019s hair. When Kiki refuses to brush her crowning glory, choosing instead to attend to more important tasks, like drawing pictures on her shoes and hands, snipping the bristles of her toothbrush and building towers out of colourful frying pans, her hair becomes a bit tangled. What starts as a small knot, grows, with some more neglect, into a giant tangled mess. Soon Kiki\u2019s chaotic hair doesn\u2019t function at all like hair normally does: it becomes her pillow and her umbrella; it acts as her schoolbag, holding all her books and stationary; it sprouts herbs to season and flavour her food; and eventually, Kiki uses her hair as a window wiper, scrubbing clean the highest windows in her house.Although this is a story about a girl\u2019s hair mishaps, the entertaining and inventive situations in which Kiki finds herself are a treat for both boys and girls. All children are likely to find appeal in spotting the bizarre things Kiki has tucked between her tufts. And at the same time, readers are taught the culinary uses of basil, thyme, oregano and mint. Written in melodic rhymes, the flow of the words, as well as the harmony between words and the delightful illustrations, make for an engaging read.It\u2019s a pity, then, that academic-style words almost certainly unknown to young readers, such as \u2018hue\u2019, \u2018boon\u2019 and \u2018expedient\u2019 interrupt the smooth flow of the story.In any case, Kiki\u2019s hair adventures are worth a read. And readers can create their own Kiki hairstyles at the back of the book.                4/11/2013 2:21:21 PM\r                            4/11/2013 2:24:19 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku reviews Pancake Pandemonium by Anita Pouroulis\xa9 2016

Pancake Pandemonium is an engaging rhyming story about Polly\u2019s attempt to create an endless supply of her favourite food, pancakes, \u201cpiping hot with butter rolling off the top, dripping with honey, all golden and runny\u201d.However, Polly\u2019s kitchen has run out of pancake ingredients, and her mom is too busy cleaning the house to replace them. So, Polly comes up with a devious plan. She pinches her mom\u2019s credit card and buys cows to use their milk, a flock of hens to provide the eggs, sacks of flour, and bees to harvest the honey she needs. But Polly realises only too late that her garden isn\u2019t the most sensible place to keep her new animals. Trouble ensues in the form of swarming bees, flour-coated chickens and a stomping, mooing, disgruntled cow.While stealing credit cards might give parents pause, Polly\u2019s wild imagination, delightfully illustrated, takes children on a whimsical journey to pancake-hoarding dragons and stacks of buttery pancakes piled to the roof. The story also familiarises young readers with the importance of cows, hens and bees in our daily lives. However, the theme of the story turns sour in the end, when Polly\u2019s pancake mayhem results in a sudden adverse reaction to pancakes, and her choosing of a menacing-looking fish pie instead. The book appears to be aimed at a middle class, British readership  - for people who are familiar with credit cards and expensive hair-removal techniques, and who readily recognise words like \u2018mum\u2019 and \u2018yard\u2019. Also, while the rhyming was endearing most of the time, it felt as if the author occasionally forced rhymes which resulted in peculiar sentence construction.Still, Pancake Pandemonium has the ingredients of an engaging story, and a tasty pancake recipe at the back.                4/11/2013 2:23:28 PM\r                            4/11/2013 2:23:28 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Matina Genkova-Mpofu's Twins\xa9 2016

Twins follows the journey of a little boy who desperately wants to understand the name we give to people who are born in pairs. \u201cWhat does \u2018twins\u2019 mean?\u201d he asks. \u201cDoes it have anything to do with mince? Maybe with beans? Or a prince? Where do twins come from? Is it from a magic case? Or some very special place?\u201dWritten in educational rhymes and dotted with cute illustrations of cars, cakes and ice-creams all in pairs, the little boy explores the meaning of \u2018twins\u2019: he explains his difficultly telling them apart, especially when they wear matching fairy outfits; he discovers that there are different types of twins, and subsequently recognises that boy-girl twins are the easiest to distinguish from one another; And finally, he learns, that although twins may look the same, they\u2019re always individually unique.Dedicated by the author \u201cto every unique twin in the world\u201d, this story is likely to guide twins on a road to realising their individuality, and to help them appreciate having someone with which to share their lives.As with Matina Genkova-Mpofu\u2019s book \u2018Mom why am I different?\u2019, the story suffers from lacking a coherent structure. While it is more likely that children think in streams of consciousness rather than in logical thought patterns, it makes the story inarticulate and difficult to follow. The little boy jumps from thinking twins are a form of food on one page, to understanding that the word refers to similar-looking people on the next page, with no apparent link. The mixture between rhyming-verse and free verse also adds to the story\u2019s disjointedness.Again, as with Matina Genkova-Mpofu\u2019s previous book, the addition of photographs gives \u2018Twins\u2019 the appearance of a pamphlet rather than a story book.While the message of the book is well-intentioned, the story would benefit from the work of an editor.                4/25/2013 12:58:53 PM\r                            4/25/2013 12:58:53 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Matina Genkova-Mpofu's Mom why am I Different?\xa9 2016

Mom why am I Different? is a heart-warming story about a little boy who suddenly realises that the colour of his skin matches neither his mother\u2019s nor his father\u2019s, and that the colours of their skin do not match one another either. \u2018Mom, why am I different?\u201d he asks.Written in the voice of a questioning child, the book explores issues of identity and colour in South Africa\u2019s Rainbow Nation by means of an entertaining experiment \u2013 the kind of explanation to which young children can easily relate. In an effort to answer her son\u2019s difficult question, the little boy\u2019s mother starts preparing a glass of hot chocolate. In the process, she compares herself to the snow-white glass of milk, the little boy\u2019s father to the dark brown cocoa powder, and then, she compares her son to the result of their combination: a scrumptious, light brown, chocolately drink.While young children will more easily relate to a book written in the voice of a young child, this voice often appears to be a series of disconnected thoughts. And while this is probably more realistic than a series of connected thoughts in a child, it makes the book hard to follow in parts.Another hodgepodge element is the pictures. They appear on the page in the style of a collage, suitable for a scrapbook. They are also not illustrations, but photographs, making the book more closely resemble a pamphlet than a story.Nevertheless, the message the book imparts is an important one: self-acceptance. It encourages readers to celebrate their individuality.                4/22/2013 3:51:34 PM\r                            4/22/2013 3:51:34 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Anita Pouroulis\u2019 My Monster Mayhem\xa9 2016

My Monster Mayhem is about a little girl with a serious problem: her house is completely infested with ghastly monsters. There is the Lumpa Loofis who hides under her bed, the Biver Quiver who lives in the drain of her bath, the Scrapadapadocus who scrounges for bits of her supper, and there\u2019s the Broncus Dentus whose favourite activity is decorating her whole bathroom in tangles of dental floss.In order to subdue these frightening pests, the little girl takes matters into her own hands: she bangs doors to frighten them, splashes bathwater to scare them away, leaves lights on to deter them, and gives them bits of her food. But these gestures trigger another problem for the little girl in the form of a very angry mother. Her mom does not approve of banging doors or slippery bathroom floors, and she expects her daughter to finish her whole plate of food!This is a lively and gorgeously illustrated rhyming story, capturing the imagination and innocence of childhood. Sentences are short and easy to read, helping to keep children engaged.Children\u2019s books are intended to work the imagination of their readers, and this book certainly achieves that. However, it might inspire the kind of imagination that gives young children nightmares. Even though the monsters do not appear to be dangerous, they look menacing, they inappropriately expose what is underneath their clothes, and a multiple-eyed ogre ends the book with a bellowing \u2018boo!\u2019It makes sense then, that the little girl is portrayed as a hero, fighting the terrifying beasts. But children should not be encouraged to hide their fears from their parents, or to deal with their worries all on their own.                4/23/2013 3:14:07 PM\r                            4/23/2013 3:14:07 PM\r

Left on his own one day, Vumile gingerly approaches the hedge where Chloe the chameleon lives, and to his delight, discovers a secret he cannot wait to share with his friends.Dotted with Xhosa words, and with references to the Bafana Bafana soccer team and \u2018goggas\u2019, this well-written and beautifully illustrated book is suitably aimed at a young South African audience.The story, although at times a little long-winded, is filled with educational facts about chameleons, as well as lessons about different cultural beliefs and overcoming fear. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Anita Pouroulis\u2019 Mum's Cronky Car\xa9 2016

Mum\u2019s cronky car is an unreliable, spluttering and downright embarrassing vehicle that, on the way to school one morning, coughs and stalls at a green robot. But what will they do about dad who is terrified of heights?Mum\u2019s Cronky Car is an exciting, magical story with delightful illustrations. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Khaya Dlanga\u2019s Cheeky In My Arrogant Opinion\xa9 2016

Khaya Dlanga is most well-known as a blogger, advertising exec and social media guru. It is probably his keen understanding of writing what people want to read and what will keep them reading, without frills or trying to sound like an intellectual, that has helped Dlanga produce such a neat and entertaining pocket book. In My Arrogant Opinion is split into three main sections: the first two are entitled \u2018The Good Black\u2019 and \u2018The Angry Black\u2019 which give a good idea of the socio-political bend of his charming and funny writing.In some places Dlanga works too hard to be controversial and he comes across as a caricature of an angry black man rather than as the insightful semi-sociologist that he is. He does at times seem, well, arrogant. At its heart though Dlanga\u2019s writing is a mirror for South Africa: self-deprecating, humorous, reflective and biting. In the best parts, it takes serious and important observations about our divided society and dresses them up as cheeky wit like this:<blockquote>The fake smile has those \u2018Please don\u2019t steal my bag\u2019 and \u2018Please don\u2019t talk to me, and don\u2019t mistake my fake smile as an invitation for you to talk to me\u2019 qualities about it. You know, I can appreciate some fake things, like fake boobs. As fake as they may seem, they look even more real than the real thing. At least they give us an idea of what those identical twins on women\u2019s chests should be like. No, don\u2019t get me wrong, I don\u2019t advocate adding any counterfeit objects to one\u2019s chest. Not that I\u2019m opposed to them either. I was just saying.</blockquote>All his bitter pills seem sugar-coated: it helps them go down easier but that does not hide what\u2019s at their core. This is why, as social commentary and a punchy look at the real \u2018new\u2019 South Africa, In My Arrogant Opinion shines. In the final section of the book, \u2018The Quotable Black\u2019, Dlanga shares many of his most tweets and quotes from Twitter in the hopes that even if he is the only person who ever quotes himself, at least he\u2019ll be quotable. For a generation of digital natives ever in search of 140 character wisdom, this short, sharp read will provide plenty of quotable quotes: Dlanga needn\u2019t have worried.                2/20/2013 12:10:15 PM\r                            2/20/2013 12:10:15 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Anele\u2019s Youngsters Pocket Book It Feels Wrong to Laugh, But\u2026.\xa9 2016

Anele Mdoda is a lot funnier than she\u2019s given credit for. In this blunt and punchy pocket book, she has ample time to share stories from her childhood, her career and her everyday life in ways that are often touching but mostly just really funny. From black women\u2019s weaves to her mother to South Africa\u2019s radio industry, nothing is off limits. There is certainly an off-beat edge to her humour which means you never really know where an anecdote is going to go and her flare for the unexpected makes for fun reading. When she describes each of South Africa\u2019s public holidays as though they were people you cannot help but laugh out loud, shaking your head a little but smiling. Mdoda is quite a character.Besides being funny, Mdoda displays an inner strength and self-belief that allows her to embrace and share her flaws, her whimsy and her failings with candor and confidence. For aspiring radio DJs, there is some advice in there for you too but it\u2019s more \u2018tough love\u2019 than \u2018keep living the dream\u2019; shedding light on an industry that seems more difficult to stay in than to get into.There are important and earnest reflections on loving yourself and doing what you love but they aren\u2019t sugar-coated and Mdoda is not interested in platitudes. This honesty is a great companion to her humour: she swears, responds to hate mail, she makes fun of the gap in her teeth and her knock knees, and in the same breath lists Grey\u2019s Anatomy and John Mayer as her gospel. With It Feels Wrong to Laugh, But\u2026., Mdoda will keep her fans happy and attract many more.                2/20/2013 12:10:07 PM\r                            2/20/2013 12:10:07 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Shaka Sisulu\u2019s Youngsters Pocket Book Becoming\xa9 2016

Shaka Sisulu\u2019s short and sharp biography is mostly a collection of his thoughts on numerous socio-political issues. The topics are all incredibly relevant for young people in South Africa today and are dealt with in an accesible and easy to digest format.Some of the book seems to be written with an international audience in mind, explaining concepts like lobola and BEE in depth that would not be necessary for most of the South Africans reading Becoming. At other times, Sisulu makes insightful arguments with subtle nuances one wishes he had the word count to explore. One example comes in the form of his belief that the World Cup was an ideal platform for improving social cohesion through organically creating spaces for South Africans to share with each other and interact with each other. While not an original concept, it was an interesting take and one hopes to see more of this creative thinking in future books.The best is saved for last with the real gems of Sisulu\u2019s humour showing in personal anecdotes in the last two chapters about some of his adventures in Oxford and the Northern suburbs of Johannesburg. While Becoming will entertain many young people, it does not offer a view of the real person behind the Twitter handle. Perhaps that was never the intention but one gets the impression that Sisulu is a man who is funny, smart and has so many more stories to tell. Sharing more of them would have made Becoming a richer and more rewarding read.                2/20/2013 12:05:11 PM\r                            2/20/2013 12:05:11 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews More of the Youngsters Series with Danny K\u2019s Take it From Me\xa9 2016

When Danny K\u2018s first single became a hit on South Africa\u2019s pop airways, it was 2000 and many of the teenagers reading Take it from Me would\u2019ve still been in nursery school. To this young audience, Danny K is most likely known as one half of the team responsible for the Shout! campaign against crime and a face more familiar from Heat than Rolling Stone magazine.In his book, Take it from Me, Danny K tries to dispel some of the misconceptions about his life and his career that have seen many \u2013 not just today\u2019s teens and tweens \u2013 consider him more of a tabloid celebrity than an actual musician. He talks about the highs and the lows of both his personal and his professional life, reflecting on the tragic death of his brother, winning his first SAMA award and his very public failed romances. He also shares the lessons he has learnt about the work and sacrifices it took to make his dreams a reality.One comes away with the impression of a down-to-earth, guarded person who after over a decade in the music industry, still feels as though he has to prove himself. This is not to say that Danny K doubts himself or his decisions but he is self-deprecating and humble in the face of his mistakes and honest about how he could have done things better.Far from being a celebrity,Danny K just seems like a normal guy: still excited about music and still burning with the same fire that has kept him working in the industry for so long. It isn\u2019t a tell-all expose of his life but it is a look backstage that will endear him to a new audience too young to remember \u2018Hurts So Bad\u2019.                2/20/2013 12:08:31 PM\r                            2/20/2013 12:08:31 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Londi Gamedze and Dorothy Dyer's From boys to men\xa9 2016

From Boys to Men follows teenager, Themba, on his path to self-discovery. After his brother and role model, Joseph, passes away, Themba feels lost and alone. These feelings are intensified when his mother decides to move to Cape Town, and Themba is faced with doing his matric year at a new school far away from his home and all of his friends. What makes matters even worse is that his mother turns to alcohol to try and deal with her grief, leaving Themba to fend for himself.But Themba\u2019s situation isn\u2019t all bad. The popular crowd befriends him at his new school, and the beautiful Lettie also takes an interest in him. When Lettie\u2019s safety is threatened by rival gangsters, Themba is faced with making the most difficult decisions of his life.This latest addition to the Harmony High series is a fast-paced and exciting read about the real-life issues facing teens. Chapters are short and the language simple, occasionally too simple, leaving readers wishing their imagination was put to some work. It is written in South African teenagers\u2019 style of speech, and makes reference to popular teen culture, making the novel accessible and engaging to its target audience.As a collaboration between Sonke Gender Justice Network and Cover2Cover Books, the story explores issues of gender-based violence, masculinity, as well as male medical circumcision as a means of avoiding the spread of HIV and AIDS. It successfully manages to tackle these tricky, at times taboo, topics in an honest and unthreatening way, subtly teaching important life lessons in the process.Themba is a real human being, with fears and faults. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews YA Historical Novel 'After the Rains'\xa9 2016

<em>Puku's guest blogger Victoria Myburgh reviews a historical novel set in a Zimbabwe long past but not forgetten.</em>Zimbabwean author <strong>Emily Barroso</strong> draws her readers into <em>After the Rains</em> so effectively that you actually feel as though you are growing up alongside tomboy \u201cJay\u201d Cameron in 1970s Rhodesia.Despite Jay's carefree, adventurous farm life, the reality is that Rhodesia is in the midst of a civil war and a fight for independence. Jay\u2019s aunt, a progressive, open-minded and free-spirited individual who assists in the movement towards freedom, threatens to cause a rift in the family and causes ever-conflicting emotions for Jay. Barroso\u2019s descriptive writing vividly creates the feeling that you are travelling in the caravan of vehicles, afraid to stop your vehicle beside the road en route to your holiday destination, for fear of attack. When your heart slows down, you are playing on the farm with Jay\u2019s best friend, Enoch, in the workers compound of the family tea farm. But then you look again and Jay is growing-up and beginning to realise that all is not as it may seem on the farm or in her country. She is torn between loyalty to her family and loyalty to her friend: ever on the lookout for \u201cthe bloody terr\u2019s\u201d and coming to the harsh realisation that her best friend may just be a part of the group that savagely attacked her mother and two young siblings on their farm. The highlight of the book was definitely the manner in which the author described the country. It was an honest description of a beautiful place that, as South Africans, we have a slightly warped and one-sided perception of. It made me really want to visit the country to see for myself.Teen readers outside of Zimbabwe might be put off that it is set during a time period that they probably understand little about but the story of Jay's journey through the same teenage struggles that we all go through is so compelling they may be surprised and excited to learn about the country's history. Like every other teenage, Jay learns about love and loss, left and right, and is finally forced to make-up her own mind in the beautiful backdrop of a country with its own growing pains, Zimbabwe.                5/23/2013 3:32:52 PM\r                            5/23/2013 3:32:52 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Cressida Cowell's The Day of the Dreader\xa9 2016

The Day of the Dreader tells the story of a deadly sea-dragon hovering near the Isle of Berk, leaving the Hooligans unable to fish for their food. While the Dreader first appears to uncurl its serpentine coils from the island and swim away from the tribe, soon, it changes its mind and makes a beeline for the terrified and hungry Hooligans.Meanwhile, Hiccup\u2019s pet dragon, Toothless, who stayed home searching for something to satisfy his growling tummy, comes across three scrumptious-looking eggs. But when he tries to cook them, they wiggle and squirm until they hatch. Suddenly, Toothless is faced with taking care of three mischievous little dragon babies who are intent on destroying Hiccup\u2019s house in their search for food. However, as Toothless and Hiccup soon discover, maybe these little terrors are not all bad. Maybe they are the answer to ridding the island of the fearful Dreader\u2026A 2012 World Book Day exclusive short story in the How to Train Your Dragon series, the book has eleven snappy chapters and is written in a delightfully humorous and easily readable style. The story is structured in such a way that readers alternate between two exciting parts: the dragon-fighting tribe and Toothless with his misbehaving dragon babies. Switching between the two effectively builds suspense and grips readers.Dotted throughout the book are cartoon-like, amusing illustrations, facts and statistics about the deathly Dreader, and a short lesson on how to speak Dragonese, a forbidden language among the Hooligans. Only the Dragon Jewel remains lost, and Hiccup Horrendous Haddock the Third has a map to where it\u2019s kept. But Hiccup is in even more trouble than most. He is an Outcast, hunted by both humans and dragons for his treasured map and sneaky antics. While doing his best to stay hidden and disguised, Hiccup is faced with, not only locating the Dragon Jewel, but also capturing the rest of the Lost Things from Alvin the Treacherous. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews YA Thriller Black Helicopters\xa9 2016

<em>Puku's Nikki McDiarmid reviews this disturbing and complex YA thriller.</em><strong>Blythe Woolston</strong> bravely attempts in <em>Black Helicopters</em> to penetrate the minds of children suicide bombers, exploring the subtle manipulation of adults over the psyche of those who have no defense systems.Through the eyes of a fifteen year old girl called Valkyrie White, or \u2018Valley\u2019, the reader is introduced to a world that appears as pure science fiction for the most part of the story.  Similar in many ways to Suzanne Collins\u2019 \u201cThe Hunger Games\u201d, a sense of the hunted and those who hunt prevails, with the so-named Black Helicopters as the evil that controls all.  Through Valley\u2019s eyes these alien creatures are mysterious killing machines and her motivation for killing is to take revenge for her mother\u2019s death.As the story progresses, it becomes more obvious to the reader, that it is Valley\u2019s understanding of the \u2018game\u2019 they play that is distorted and twisted. The girl herself is the hunter.  The first chapter is rather abstract and may put off readers as too many secondary characters vie for attention and names are too numerous to remember.  Opening sentences such as \u201cThe men have become my legs and I\u2019m a twelve-foot-tall, four legged girl because that\u2019s what I need to be\u201d were confusing rather than enlightening.  However, push through, because by chapter three the reader is well and truly hooked.Written simply, in an easy-to-read style, the story flows well and contains some powerful twists that keep the suspense throughout, making for a real page turner of a book.  Added bonuses were the police reports that not only broke the flow of Blythe Woolston\u2019s writing, but added depth to the story and drew out the suspense.Perhaps the strangest part of this book was the \u2018voice\u2019 of Valley, which is played in juxtaposition against her real age. As she speaks, Valley\u2019s \u2018voice\u2019 displays the stunted mental and emotional growth of the girl, carrying with it the quirks and fascinations of a much younger child.  References to Dr. Seuss\u2019s \u201cFox in Socks\u201d were delightful, enforcing Valley\u2019s youth and innocence for the reader. This young, captive adult, known to the reader as an immature child, at times made for terribly confusing reading.  Blythe Woolston has depicted Valley so well, that the reader does indeed fall into the trap of forgetting how old she really is. Instead one begins to see her as a child of seven or eight. It is when the reader is reminded of Valley\u2019s real age that this twist makes the story profoundly disturbing.The descriptions of inner madness and the darkness of the girl\u2019s mental instability are shocking on their own, but played against the innocence and vulnerability of the character, the horror of this story moved quickly from a Young Adult story to Adult Fiction.  It is here that the sexual overtones, only hinted at in the beginning, explode and become rather intense.Having read <em>Black Helicopters</em> directly after the Boston Marathon Bombing, the subject matter was thought-provoking and for weeks after, I was still pondering the ins and outs of human instability.  This is a well-written story with the ability to truly haunt the reader. However, when a chapter makes me as an adult gasp, then I must question the suitability of this story for 14 year olds and suggest that readers be selective in their choices.                6/4/2013 8:13:50 AM\r                            6/4/2013 8:13:50 AM\r

Right from the start the battle between good and evil commences as Angelo, or \u2018Lo as brother Eric fondly calls him, slowly unravels the mysteries surrounding the approach of his 18th birthday. <br><br> In the quiet, small town where everybody knows their neighbour, it soon becomes evident that the Dimitrov family harbours many secrets. Daniel Dimitrov is a distant father to his three sons. He is of no help and offers no support when Angelo (the youngest of three brothers), experiences the wrath of school bullies, a feeling of not fitting in, peer pressure and unkind teachers. The topic of school bullies has been very prominent in the media of late and should sit well with young adult readers. <br><br>In contrast to their father, Angelo\u2019s mother and brothers Eric and Leon, seem almost over protective of him, even more so closer to his birthday. Ultimately he simply wants to be a normal boy but it seems as if this is not to be as he is crudely pushed into the direction where life needs him the most.  <br><br>A world of angels and demons, messengers and decipherers and even a \u2018canine of Torment\u2019 are cleverly woven into this tale. It\u2019s all about being brave and making the right decision, however difficult and sad the ultimate sacrifice may be. Angelo\u2019s life story is one which young readers will relate to: trustworthy friends become his second family, he falls in love and experiences the same fears, hopes and dreams of any 18 year old. But the unusual responsibility that falls on the youngster\u2019s shoulders seems too hard to bear at times. How does one make these important decisions if you haven\u2019t yet figured out what life is all about for yourself? There are horrific clashes between Light and Darkness in order to win Angelo\u2019s ultimate vote which could have far reaching implications for mankind.<br><br>The story flows well and I didn\u2019t want to put the book down. There are some predictable events but the writer makes up for this with enough intrigue and suspense to keep the reader\u2019s interest. Although Angelo was different and cast out in his community as a child, others identified that he was special too and quite able to face the challenges that Light and Darkness presented him with. I found this an intriguing aspect many teenagers could relate to. The ending of \u201call\u2019s not always so well that ends well\u201d left the door open for a follow up and I for one, can\u2019t wait!</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br>                6/10/2013 7:41:27 AM\r                            6/10/2013 7:42:08 AM\r

<em>Puku's Nikki McDiarmid reviews Nollybooks' More than Friends.</em>Neo is a grade one teacher who lives at home in the township with her mother and aunt.  Fiercely passionate about South Africa, Neo is involved with the local Community Forum, who aims to stop the destruction of the Leetong informal settlement. Good-looking and successful, Nceba\u2019s presence forces Neo to question her relationship with her boyfriend and to reassess her priorities.<br><br>The South African flavours are delightfully refreshing - familiar images of busy squatter camps are combined with well-known scents of Red Door perfume and wafting pap and chicken stew.The dialogue is wonderful and transports the reader to a place of intimate private discussions that bare all. <em>Professor Fungus en die skrikwekkende snotzilla resensie deur Puku se Madelein du Toit</em>Professor Fungus en die Skrikwekkende Snotzilla is 40 bladsye van pure lekker lees (of voorlees). Ek het met tye geskater van die lag vir Edison (die robot) met sy snaakse robot taal en Argon die konyn wat soos \u2018n hond blaf. Professor Fungus is hard besig in sy laboratorium om \u2018n vrotvoet formule te maak wat stink tekkies in \u2018n japtrap sommer weer vars sal laat ruik. Maar sy verkoue maak dit moeilik om te konsentreer en as hy nies oor die formule groei daar \u2018n groot groen blop uit die fles: Snotzilla word gebore! En nou moet die konyn, die robot en die kinders saamspan om die verkoue monster te stuit.Die tweeling Benny en Jenny karring gedurig aan mekaar nes \u2018n boetie en sussie maar doen, maar altwee van hulle is baie lief vir Professor Fungus en sy interessante eksperimente. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Word play and family fun with new picture book\xa9 2016

<em>Puku's Nikki McDiarmid reviews an unusual educational picture book</em>.Based on the concept of left- and right-brained activity, the aim of the Fabuwords books is to introduce homophones with representative images to children in order to cement the words, their meaning and spelling for young learners.In Grade Two, word lists contain \u2018son\u2019 and \u2018sun\u2019, or \u2018fur\u2019 and \u2018fir\u2019, with the emphasis on using them correctly.  \u2018To\u2019, \u2018too\u2019 and \u2018two\u2019 are enough to confuse even the brightest young mind and by Grade Three, with different sounds coming into play, spelling lists can become overwhelming. \u2018Pharmacy\u2019 and \u2018pharynx\u2019 versus \u2018enough\u2019 and \u2018trough\u2019, while \u2018trawl\u2019 and \u2018drawing\u2019 play against \u2018gaunt\u2019 and \u2018fauna\u2019.Homophones such as \u2018mall\u2019 and \u2018maul\u2019 or \u2018flaw\u2019 and \u2018floor\u2019 become difficult test words and one cannot help wondering, what happens when children get to words like \u2018wait\u2019 and \u2018weight\u2019, or \u2018cite\u2019, \u2018site\u2019 and \u2018sight\u2019? Fabuwords picture books could well begin a love for homophones, with the simple designs and age related buzz words and symbols instantly recognizable to children.So conditioned are we to the visual impact of billboards and advertising, that at first these simple pages did little to engage the senses of the family.  However, through group interaction, the images became meaningful and entertaining and the game was on.Family members reacted to different aspects of both homophones and images, alternating in understanding, regardless of age.  Who could make out the image first and who would remember the correct spelling?My nine year old immediately noticed the street sign in \u2018to\u2019 and the compass in \u2018here\u2019. The number \u2018two\u2019, with stylized faces drinking from two straws in two milkshakes, very cleverly reminded him that this word is spelt with a \u2018w\u2019.  A week later, when tested, the images were still clear in my son\u2019s memory and his spelling was accurate.The only worry I did have, was with the pages for \u2018sun\u2019 and \u2018son\u2019, the first self-explanatory as the \u2018u\u2019 melts into a stylized yellow circle edged with blue sky. The word \u2018son\u2019 however, had an image of a motorbike. While this makes little sense to representing a \u2018son\u2019, it is the words below that explain a father taking his son to look at fast wheels, for wheels are round and \u2018son\u2019 has an \u2018o\u2019 in comparison to a \u2018u\u2019. This seemed a little ambiguous because wheels don\u2019t suggest a \u2018son\u2019 - neither do balls or bicycles.  We as a family found that this confused, rather than helped, the homophone.And yet, as a visual learner, I was intrigued with the Fabuwords concept.  With each child so different to the next, tools like this are essential for the junior primary student.  I await the next book eagerly.                7/4/2013 6:36:08 AM\r                            7/4/2013 6:36:08 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Boy Band Adventure, Fallen Star\xa9 2016

<em>Puku's Madelein du Toit reviews a humours and touching novel about broken dreams and rebuilding you life.</em>The cover and title of Ian Barker\u2019s debut novel should hint at its story content. It is a modern story, a tale within a tale, following a boy band on their rise and fall. The young members of the Fallen Boys are exposed to the harsh world that accompanies fame: sex, drugs and financial manipulation by agents. The fast pace in the world of the popular boy band is intertwined with groupies (who are willing to sacrifice their souls for an evening spent with a member of the band), a free flow of drugs and alcohol and an unscrupulous agent for whom the band is just another business transaction. Ethics and morals are vague or non-existent to the band members at the peak of their fame.It is a modern story told in the language used by present day young people. Although this is necessary to keep the novel in touch with reality, the cursing initially bothered me. As the story develops the expletives fall away and is only used for those characters whose personalities cannot do without it.  In the beginning I also felt uncomfortable at times with the masturbation, casual sex, drugs and alcohol abuse of the band members. This is \u2018young adult\u2019 literature, after all. But, as the story unfolds, Karl takes stock of his life and realises that fame is a fantasy world totally out of touch with reality. The aspects which I found bothersome actually serves as  foundation for the unfolding novel.When the band folds and Karl falls in love with Lizzie he is forced to re-evaluate his life. There is also the problem of their families who are on opposite political sides of a troubled Ireland and some minor conflicts appear through the novel until the families agree to let bygones be bygones for the sake of their children. The subtle juxtaposition of the meaning of fame and the meaning of being a war hero is quite thought provoking.I am hesitant to recommend the book to readers younger than 15. Older readers may relate more to the fleeting fame of up-and-coming rock bands and the eventual re-evaluation of morals and ethics once the popularity fades. As a matter of fact, there are quite a few situations in the book that will leave the reader mulling it over somewhat. A modern story with many facets which should appeal and be relevant to the present generation.                7/4/2013 6:45:06 AM\r                            7/4/2013 6:45:06 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Darkly Enchanting Black Spring\xa9 2016

<em>Black Spring was reviewed by Puku's Nikki Mcdiarmid:</em>So rarely does a book enchant with its language and verse alone.  Alison Croggon\u2019s writing is wonderful and the lilt of her words flow deliciously through the mind as the story unfolds.  Hammel is a young man who finds the city unbearable, so he leaves town for the Northern Plateau, at the base of the Black Mountains.  Cherry House belongs to a member of royalty, who never uses it and placed on the \u2018Land of Death\u2019 and vendetta, the walls have their own tale to tell.In many different to the city, Hammel soon discovers that the North follows the old traditions, where Wizards, Priests and Kings exist together under the same laws.  New to his surroundings, Hammel picks up on strange occurrences, feels the changes in attitudes and senses an air of magic around him.  In order to pay his respects, the young man seeks out the owner of the house, Damek, only to discover a character, who seems completely mad.  Not only is Damek unlikeable and violent, but the house is filled with ghosts and a witch called Lina.It is only when he returns to Cherry house, that Anna the housekeeper, becomes the narrator of a story so mysterious, that Hammel will never be the same again.Peculiar and intriguing, Black Spring is reminiscent of <em>Inkheart</em> by Cornelia Funke, where the suspense is wrapped up in the mysterious and the reader is required to solve the puzzles actively, rather than passively reading.  Charles Dicken\u2019s <em>Great Expectations</em> too, comes to mind, in both the writing style and within the living conditions and stations of a traditional Victorian household.Written in the first person narrative, the initial chapters are difficult to follow and restrictive on the author and the reader.Hammel\u2019s backstory is confusing in the attempts to explain his process to the point of leaving for Cherry House, as time lines get lost in the changing tenses.  The first few chapters need to be read more than once to be properly understood.However, while the story is bizarre, with some gaps that leave characters floundering, YA readers who are fond of good prose will enjoy Black Spring immensely.The characters are incredibly well detailed and the suspense keeps the reader hanging on for more.  Magic in this world is underlying and quietly spoken of, and yet perhaps, it has even more power in the silence.I personally enjoyed the play on the different beliefs that exist together, with Wizards of great darkness agreeing with the Priests of the Church, while Royalty keeps them all under wraps.  This system of governance, in a world disappointed by its leaders, is most convincing and something young readers will certainly respond to.No matter how abstract the story gets, the writing makes Black Spring difficult to put down and is a book I would most certainly read again and again.                7/23/2013 7:36:36 AM\r                            7/23/2013 7:36:36 AM\r

En ek was jaloers op die digters wat my eie tienerjare so nommerpas kon beskryf. Met tye het ek teveel ge\xefdentifiseer met van die verse en het \u2018n ou, bekende tienerseer sy verskyning gemaak. Ek was verbaas want ek het gereken ek het gedeel daarmee ...Jaco Jacobs het hierdie verse vir tieners saamgestel en weereens gewys hoe knus hy die w\xeareld van die moderne jongmens meeleef. Rebellie, woede, onsekerheid, verliefdheid, speelsheid, hartseer - die tipiese sturm und drang van tienerlewe. Die moderne taal van die tiener word voluit gebruik, maar met baie respek vir Afrikaans en dit is so lekker.   Tieners tussen 13 en 15 jaar oud is nog vis  nog vlees en vind dit moeilik om hulle voete te vind, enige plek. Maar dis ook \u2018n wonderlike tyd van krap, soek en ondervind en beleef. \u2018n Bitter soet tyd in \u2018n mens se lewe. Om \u2018n obsessie oor iemand te h\xea mag radikaal klink, maar dit is net wat dit is \u2013 \u2018n wonderlike, mooi seun/man oorskadu jou alledaagse doen en late en alhoewel jy nie self jou gevoelens verstaan nie, weet jy net jy sal waaragtig \u201c... jou girlfriends gif gee\u201d om sy liefde te wen. Jy draai sy naam uit elke appelstingel, fantaseer en beleef die eerste soen elke minuut van die dag, en as sy vir jou glimlag geniet jy sommer Maandae by die skool en kry jou tokse vlerke op die rugbyveld. Dis \u2018n gesukkel om \u201cIn die wiskundeklas\u201d te konsentreer want niks maak meer \u201csin\u201d nie.  Ek het self die vrese ervaar van \u2018n nuwe skool en die harde baklei om jou plek te kry tussen die \u201cvlekvrye-staal-smiles\u201d. Ek onthou die klok se lui en die onsekerheid van waarheen om te gaan, die herinneringe laat my hart  nou nog krimp. Hoe pas \u2018n mens in tussen die \u201cEksieperfeksie\u201d gesin? Het jy al lus gevoel om uit pure frustrasie jou ma se \u201cstrelitzia\u201d om te skop? En daardie vreeslike alleen gevoel op \u201cSondagaande\u201d ... Ek speel nou rond met frases uit 54 verse, want daar is doodgewoon nie plek om al die digters erkenning te gee nie. Die voorblad van die boek illustreer die balanseer toertjie van tiener wees, die loop op die tou waar alles in jou w\xeareld met \u2018n \u201cte\u201d vooraan geleef word. Te vinnig, te stadig, te passievol, te emosioneel, te verlief, te maer, te vet ...  <em>\u201cek hou my oog op die tou<br>ek balanseer my lewe<br>en voet vir voet skuif ek vorentoe<br>deur die storm\u201d</br></br></br></em>(touloper \u2013 Fanie Viljoen)Dit was vir my lekker om Toulopers te lees en ek weet sommer Sondae, Facebook, sokker en tiener-blues gaan my altyd herinner aan hierdie bundel!                 8/5/2013 7:11:31 AM\r                            8/5/2013 7:11:31 AM\r
