[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Nikki McDiarmid reviews "Thirteen"\xa9 2016

<img alt="Markings and boy" src="http://images.kalahari.net/img/2013/08/27/47716784_0_Img2.jpg"/><strong>Tom Hoyle\u2019s</strong> chilling thriller will have young adults riveted while feeling a little afraid to turn the lights out at night.Amidst the sounds of New Year celebrations, a young girl in the maternity ward is giving birth to her first child. A boy who is born on the stroke of midnight.  Meanwhile, a few floors below them, a man enters the hospital wielding a sword and slaughtering all who stand in his way as he seeks to fulfill a prophecy to save the world.With no choice but to run, the girl searches the city for help, finally leaving her baby on the steps of a building, and in doing so, saves the child\u2019s life, as her own is taken.Thirteen years later, Adam lives a normal life with a loving family, but he is being stalked by killers.<em>\u2018The People\u2019</em> are following the prophecy that says Adam must be killed before he turns fourteen.  With adults, children and uniformed infiltrators, the hunters could be anyone, anywhere.  Adam must survive, even when his own family are attacked and his house set alight.A tale fit for Halloween this story is about hooded monsters waiting in the shadows to pray upon the innocent.<strong>Thirteen</strong> is not a quick read.  Intense and suggestive, the prologue immediately has readers questioning where the threads will lead them; to witchcraft, mental sickness or perhaps religious fanaticism.As the role of the priviledged, suburban school boy is compared to the lives of children brainwashed into religious duty, the intensity increases.  The chapters tick slowly at first, like a pendulum that swings, getting faster and faster, until the reader is left breathless.<strong>Hoyle</strong> does not hold back from using extreme shock tactics and he sugar-coats nothing, yet all the while keeping the wording simple and free from any actual description.  Instead, the reader's mind is pushed beyond their own parameters of fear.<strong>Thirteen</strong> could be likened to a suburban <strong><em>\u2018Lord of the Flies\u2019</em></strong>, made even more chilling by the child on child violence.  As an adult I was disturbed and found the plot almost too hard and too cruel, constantly questioning how this psychotic aggression would affect a younger reader.  For me this was almost a rendition of <strong><em>'The Hunger Games'</em></strong> taken that one step further.And yet, high school stories must always be about the two sides, a precarious balance of enjoyable growth experiences mixed with the relevant fears of bullying and power.  Children today face so much more than we did and this story unmasks some of the fear South African children live with constantly.<strong>Thirteen</strong> is thought provoking and will have readers on the edge of their seats.  It is a book I was unable to put down and would be nervous to open again.<strong>Play the Game:</strong>http://www.panmacmillan.com/News/February-2014/Thirteen-Instagram-adventure<strong>To buy the book:</strong>http://www.kalahari.com/Books/Thirteen<em>p</em>47716784                3/6/2014 8:47:16 AM\r                            5/6/2014 7:40:03 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Nikki McDiarmid reviews Supernova magazine 2.3 2013\xa9 2016

<img alt="Magazine cover: The busy life of bees, Supernova" src="https://s-media-cache-ak0.pinimg.com/236x/eb/60/cf/eb60cf5483762f05cc5e26ddfa0c6739.jpg"/><strong>Supernova is excellent reading for children aged nine and up.</strong>With articles on flora and fauna, pollination, bees, moon cycles and \u2018fracking\u2019 systems in mining, these pages go hand in hand with the CAPS government curriculum and are filled with colloquialisms that make reading all South African.Other countries feature too, in this case Ireland, with a delightful five pages of facts, fiction, legends and history, while articles include sports, environmental issues, career awareness and music, just to name a few. The diversity is exciting and ensures that any child will find a topic to hold their interest.Similar only to the Afrikaans magazine Hoezit by ATKV, Supernova covers everything proudly South African from land to sea, exploring the country and it's people.This magazine so brilliantly fits the divide between adult publications and those for children, creating a perfect balance between the two. Every detail is noticed, right down to placing a cartoon next to the magazine introduction letter, something children seldom read.To begin with, the pages of Supernova are rather overwhelming for youngsters.Compared to similar publications, Supernova includes an incredible amount of writing, but this is a good thing. The challenge is necessary to moving up the ladder of reading.With a little help, my nine year-old was soon used to the smaller font and reading the articles on his own. He participated avidly in discussion and was confident enough to compare the information he had already learnt at school with what he had discovered from the magazine.Impressively, it took thirty pages of intensive reading before he took a break. With another twenty pages to go, the entertainment is guaranteed to be informative as well as fun.<strong>The most exciting difference was the inclusion of a sport section.</strong> Wakeboarding was explained in this volume, but children\u2019s accessibility to local heroes and sports stars is another area sadly lacking in children\u2019s reading matter. Articles on the intensive rules of cricket, the pressures of soccer or how swimming clubs work could also be most valuable to children who are choosing to take them in school.<strong>Coverage on our own South African heroes</strong>Stories on true South African heroes, such as actors, playwrights, singers, sports stars and national heroes can only enhance our children\u2019s desire to read, and while the comic pages are a great combination of design and story, the need to encourage a child's imagination is just as important as their understanding of general knowledge.Jam-packed with quality articles and striking pictures, the glossy pages are not only wonderful for reading, but for help with projects and speeches. Reaching a wide audience, the Supernova Magazine raises the level of children\u2019s reading matter already on the shelves by introducing the vibrant flavours of South Africa. Dit is die realistiese manier waarop hierdie storie vertel word wat hard slaan. Troeteldiere sal net so wel-af wees soos die geaardhede van hul eienaars sal toelaat. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">'Wild Africa Tamed' by Trevor Webster\xa9 2016

<img alt="Giraffe drinking" src="http://ecx.images-amazon.com/images/I/51r3h+fEEUL.jpg"/><strong>Reviewed by PUKU's Madelein du Toit.</strong>Author and journalist <strong>Trevor Webster</strong> has travelled extensively throughout his life observing Africa\u2019s wild life. His passion was fuelled when he had to visit central and southern Africa in 1956-57 while working for the Rhodesian Customs and Excise Service.This resulted in an annual one month sabbatical of sorts in Southern Africa for the past twenty years. During this time Trevor Webster has written children\u2019s nonsense poetry in his quest to provide information about Africa\u2019s animals.<em>'Wild Africa Tamed'</em> is his latest offering.The poems are light, informing and at times put a smile on one\u2019s face. It is clear that the author is well travelled, and has observed and researched wild animals extensively. His tone of voice is affectionate and filled with wonder at nature: termites are <em>\u201cMaster Builders\u201d</em> and he describes Weavers as <em>\u201cNot So Sociable\u201d</em> as well as including,<em>\u201cTheir nests are secured by a cunning knot,</em><em>So they can move about and stay on one spot!\u201d</em>The paperback cover boasts the wonderful photograph of a giraffe drinking at a small water pond, with some gemsbok (Oryx gazelle) feeding in the background. A tranquil African scene promising a good read.This, however, is where the imagery ends.<img alt="Bulbul Bird" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Brown-eared_Bulbul_1.jpg/260px-Brown-eared_Bulbul_1.jpg"/>When I reached \u201c<em>The Pirate of the Skies</em>\u201d (on page 43) I felt like crying.The bulbul bird is exquisitely beautiful, but like so many other pages before, there was no picture or image to accompany and describe the content of the poem. Not even a childlike drawing as presented thus far with most of the poems. Such a pity.  In the age of fingertip technology and the ease with which youngsters can access the world, it is my fear that they might find Wild Africa Tamed a bit bland. African and South African young adults in particular are blessed to grow up with firsthand knowledge and wonderful photographs of the animals roaming Africa.Readers of 12+ will probably enjoy the book, but I suspect once they have read or paged through it, it will most probably not be afforded a second glance.<hr/><img alt="Bookchat logo" src="http://www.bookchat.co.za/images/img-green.jpg"/><h2>Bookchat's Jay Heale:</h2>Aptly subtitled \u201cInformative Verse\u201d, this London publication squeezes in because (apart from a few odd bits at the end) these verses are all about South African wildlife. Jauntily written, they include a lot of accurate animal details.Fun to read with a bit of education along the line. Here\u2019s the beginning of one of them:<em>"The vulture is the dustman on the hill,</em><em>Tidying up when others have eaten their fill."</em>                4/28/2014 10:14:32 AM\r                            4/28/2014 10:49:24 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Cape Town\u2019s Ani Mallover\u2019s Debut, Miranda The Polkadot Panda\xa9 2016

Miranda the Polkadot Panda is an endearing story about a little panda called Miranda. Miranda has polka dots on her stomach which makes her very different and quite unique but when a cruel new friend makes fun of her, Miranda is devastated and runs away from home. Only a crazy old bird can help Miranda see that she\u2019s perfect as she is \u2013 and so is everyone else. Can Miranda learn to accept the spots that make her different? Can she find her way home to her worried mommy, Amanda? Readers will just have to wait and see.The book is a bit long for the 6-10 year old market at 81 pages but it is split into 13 chapters so it can be read in parts. The entire story is written in Dr Seuss-esque rhyming couplets and while the rhyming is cute and clever in parts, after the first few chapters it begins to feel tedious. More than that, it also comes across as forced rather than whimsical in some of the dialogue.The highlight of Miranda the Polkadot Panda is certainly the beautiful illustrations. They are rich, colourful and textured to the extent that you could imagine a child wanting to spend the book just looking at the pictures and skimming through the text. The cover illustrations have an inspired glossy finish that turn an ordinary book into something that feels silky and special to the touch.Miranda the Polkadot Panda will probably make a good bedtime story: two chapters a night for a week with keep all the little pandas wanting more. Let\u2019s hope Ani Mallover\u2019s next book showcases one of Africa\u2019s endangered species next.                2/27/2013 9:06:19 PM\r                            2/27/2013 9:06:19 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Cape Town Caper Hotwire\xa9 2016

Ben is genuinely good guy just trying to do well in school for his single mom, make whatever pocket money he can from washing cars and try to learn something useful at school without getting stabbed. As a coloured boy from an under-resourced school, growing up in a white suburb of Cape Town, he has to deal with racism, gangsterism and the stereotypes pushed on him. His friend, Tarif, has to deal with a different set of problems entirely with a brother who has disappeared into a secretive and independent life and a sister rebelling against their Muslim traditions and finding a new boyfriend every week.Ben ultimately just wants to be a teenager, learning how to use computers better and talking about cars and girls with his best friend Tarif, but adults in his life make staying a child difficult as their problems make Ben and Tarif\u2019s life more complicated than they though possible in primary school. They\u2019re coming of age in a dangerous time and a dangerous environment and they have to keep their wits about them if they want to make it out of high school and into the big wide world.The two young heroes are believable, funny and honest. The book touches on issues of inequality, crime, drifting apart from our siblings and trying to help our parents when no one else can. Both boys and girls in their early teens will enjoy Ben and Tarif\u2019s Southern suburbs adventure through Cape Town. Hotwire is a quick and fun read: enjoy the ride!                2/27/2013 9:13:29 PM\r                            2/27/2013 9:13:29 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews Cover2Cover\u2019s First Teen Novel Broken Promises\xa9 2016

<em>Puku's winter intern, Nonkululeko Zondo, recently read the Cover2Cover teen drama, Broken Promises:</em>Broken Promises follows the story of Ntombi who has to deal with the new man in her mother\u2019s life, Zakes, who has changed their family dynamic. She has to do all of this while juggling rehearsals for South Africa\u2019s \u2018Teen Voice\u2019 auditions and Mzi, one of the cutest guys in matric, who has taken a sudden interest in her. Zakes is dangerous and not to be trusted but Ntombi\u2019s mother won\u2019t hear of it. Ntombi\u2019s discovery of Mzi\u2019s connection with Zakes casts some doubts on Mzi\u2019s sincerity and places Ntombi in a great deal of danger.The book is an easy read with a plot and characters that would definitely appeal to younger audiences. The themes and issues of the book are certainly relevant to a teen living in modern South Africa and bring a dimension of reality to it. I particularly enjoyed the point where Mzi confronts Ntombi about her spying on him and Zakes at Mama\u2019s Tavern. At this point Olwethu proves himself an unlikely hero and comes through for her just before Mzi strikes.The story itself does not allow for much character development or change however Ntombi does learn not to accept everything at face value and to trust her instincts. The other characters are mainly flat characters that contribute to the plot\u2019s progression and help Ntombi learn some lessons and learn about herself and discover her inner strength. The most important of these, in my opinion, is Priscilla, a girl she meets at Thabiso\u2019s Tavern who leads Ntombi to question Mzi and his motives for being with her.It wouldn\u2019t have been remiss to have more indigenous language words interspersed between the English to make the characters more believable and reflective of the diversity of Ntombi\u2019s community, especially if English isn\u2019t their first language. Despite this, it was an enjoyable and engaging book that would certainly hold the attention of its target audience.                2/27/2013 9:30:14 PM\r                            2/27/2013 9:30:14 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Nikki McDiarmid reviews "Rock War"\xa9 2016

<img alt="Boy with guitar" src="https://www.hachettechildrens.co.uk/assets/HachetteChildrensBooks/img/book/581/isbn9781444920581-detail.jpg"/><strong>Robert Muchamore\u2019s</strong> books are bestsellers with teenagers around the world, his new Rock War series recently picked as one of ten titles for World Book Day 2014.<strong><em>Rock War: The Audition</em></strong> was written as a \u2018mini-book\u2019 especially for World Book Day and will be available for just \xa31 in the UK and as a bargain priced e-book in other countries.  In South Africa this e-book is priced at R14,68.Sadie, Noah and Chris are friends who used to play music together in a band called the Unicorns.  With Noah on guitar, Sadie on drums and lyrics, and Chris on keyboard, the Unicorns did pretty well until Chris fell in love.  Now, having not practiced together for a year and unofficially broken up, Noah sees his dreams slipping away from him.That is until he finds a competition audition that might get him into the Rock War Academy.  As a talented guitarist, he stands a good chance except for one problem.  The audition requirement is that the bands entering have four members.  No more and no less.When the school \u2018cool kids\u2019 also want to enter, but are short a bass player, they ask Noah to help them out.  It is an abrupt change in relationships as the tables are turned.Noah must weigh up his desire to win the Audition Show, over taking revenge on the bullies he has a history with.  Confined to a wheelchair, Noah may have enough talent to win, but can he handle the crass and abusive Fergal who has made his life so very difficult?  And is it worth leaving his friends behind to have a shot at his dreams?While Noah learns that being in a wheelchair makes you different, perhaps that is exactly what the world is looking for, but will it be what he really wants?<strong><em>Rock War: The Audition</em></strong> is a quick and easy read that will inspire both young musicians and readers.<br/>The bullies are really mean and the reader can\u2019t help cheering Noah on as he gets his own back.Suitable for ages twelve and up, parents just need to be aware of the language content and aggressive bullying for sensitive readers.<img alt="World Book Day banner" src="http://www.worldbookday.com/wp-content/themes/twentyeleven_wbd/images/logo.png"/>                5/8/2014 7:04:47 AM\r                            5/8/2014 7:04:47 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Puku Reviews the Explosive Too Young to Die\xa9 2016

Too Young to Die is the latest book in Cover2Cover\u2019s Harmony high series and the best one so far. For whatever reason, we don\u2019t often get young adult books from a boy\u2019s perspective. The YA genre is currently dominated by romance, historical romance, dystopia with a post-apocalyptic love interest, sparkling vampires and shirtless werewolves. This book was exciting because, for a change, it gave a voice to a real-life, contemporary, South African boy.The action starts right away: Mzi and his partner in crime Zakes have been shoved into the back of a police van, arrested after a spree of carjackings. They are on their way to court where Zakes will surely be sentenced to jail time and Mzi feels dizzy and sick thinking about how he got himself into so much trouble. His glamorous criminal lifestyle, buying beers for all the boys at the tavern, flashing his gun to anyone who challenged him and having all the girls throw themselves at him, comes to an abrupt end when Mzi is essentially put on probation with community service in lieu of jail time. For the first time in a long time, he has to be good \u2013 going to school every day and staying home at night.It\u2019s a far cry from his former life and all the respect he earned as a thug evaporates. The boys at school make fun of him, the girls giggle and point. He is the joke of Harmony High and he blames Olwethu, his ex-girlfriend\u2019s new boyfriend, for it all. To earn back his peers\u2019 respect, Mzi knows he has to get revenge. He tries to push down all his feelings of fear, empathy, rejection and guilt and focus only on his anger which is easier to deal with but which leads him down a much more dangerous path.Mzi is a compelling male character, not just a mysterious, broody young man and not just someone\u2019s love interest. Through his thoughts, we also get a glimpse into what drives young criminals in the townships. Mzi faces an equally difficult road to revenge as he does to redemption and it\u2019s not clear until the last few pages which he will actually choose. Too Young to Die is filled with the raw aggression of a young man trapped in a life he doesn\u2019t want and the twists and turns in Mzi\u2019s quest to get even with Olwethu will keep both male and female young readers guessing right until the emotionally charged, explosive ending.                2/27/2013 9:35:13 PM\r                            2/27/2013 9:35:13 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Nikki McDiarmid reviews 'Bobby'\xa9 2016

<img alt="girl with dog" src="http://elizabethpienaar.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/03/Bobby-cover-revised.jpg"/><h2>A Story of Heartache and Love.</h2><em>Bobby</em> is a handsome German Shepherd who narrates the dramatic tale of his own life as a dog in Johannesburg.Experiencing major upheavals when his owner is murdered, <em>Bobby</em> is passed from one family to another, his journey both heartbreaking and deeply touching.  As he searches for another meaningful connection with a human, <em>Bobby\u2019s</em> life runs parallel to that of a teenage girl, Jessie. Grieving for a pet she has lost, Jessie\u2019s heart is broken and her life is deemed not worth living.Destined to save each other, both are on a pilgrimage fraught with gut-wrenching hardships and terrible choices, so brilliantly written that the reader is swept along on a tide of emotion and love.No matter how difficult this story for some, <em>Bobby</em> is impossible to put down and so very beautiful to read.We are poignantly reminded of why animal friendships are so necessary, for in essence they teach us about our own humanity. Profoundly confrontational on every level, <strong>Elizabeth Pienaar</strong> holds nothing back as she raises questions that make us look harder at ourselves - as a people, as a culture and as a nation.Keeping the story simple, but remaining honestly brutal, <strong>Pienaar</strong> exposes the shocking treatment that dogs endure in this country, making the reader ask \u201c<em>Where is South Africa\u2019s compassion?\u201d</em>Do we weep for our animals or do we weep for ourselves?  With stories of abuse everywhere, are South Africans able to face them, or do we really turn away?  Shouldn\u2019t we be teaching our children to deal with them too?  For how else do we change the mind-set of a nation gone astray?While the reading of this tale is almost cathartic, it cuts to the very core.  Ultimately, the pain of owning a pet is in both loving and losing them, but the greatest blessing in life is the love that comes with accepting that loss and <strong>Elizabeth Pienaar</strong> unfolds this balance with the most delicate care.There is no better way to teach our children compassion for animals and South Africans should request <em>Bobby</em> as a set work, explored not only by students, but also by their parents and educators.Challenging our understanding of animals while at the same time helping us to reconnect, this tale is for anyone who has loved a dog.<em>Bobby</em> will change you forever.<h2>The Author:</h2><em>Elizabeth Pienaar is a Johannesburg-based writer, architect and mother. She has attended the Caine Prize Workshop in Kenya in 2007, and Breadloaf Writer\u2019s Conference in the USA in 2012.</em><em>She has published online in  International Pen Magazine (2006), African Writing Online (2008), and Sentinel Literary Quarterly (2011)and her short stories have appeared in the anthologies African Compass (2005), African Roads (2006), Jungfrau and other Short Stories (2007), Open (2008),  and Minttutoffeen onni / The Pleasures of Peppermints, a Finnish-English collaboration (2012).</em>                5/14/2014 8:13:23 AM\r                            5/24/2014 4:27:52 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">PUKU se Madelein du Toit resenseer Apie se magie\xa9 2016

Ben is \u2018n skaaphond wat na drie verlore skapies moet soek. Hy kry vir Lina en Lisa, maar waar sou Lika tog wees? Die leser vingervoel nou die paadjie tot by \u2018n flappie wat oopgemaak moet word. Kruip Lika dalk daar onder weg? Apie se magie gor sommer van die honger. Waar kan hy iets kry om te eet? Volg die paadjie met jou vinger tot by die deurtjies op die bladsy, miskien sal Apie daaragter iets te ete kry. Langs die pad kry die leser talle diertjies waaroor daar lekker vertel en geleer kan word. Beide boekies is van kleurvolle, dik karton gemaak wat dit maklik hanteer maak vir lomp handjies. Die blaaie kan ook met \u2018n klam lappie afgevee word na vele hantering. Ek voorspel dat kinders die verrassingselement van die opligdeurtjies baie gaan geniet en daarom twyfel ek aan die leeftyd van die flappies. [<div class="span8"><p class="lead">The Stanley Family review Supernova's 3.2 2014 edition\xa9 2016

<img alt="Elf with moon behind" src="http://www.magazines.co.za/uploads/medium/054999001386168453.jpg"/>With articles on sharks, religions, geocaching, flash mobs, DJ Kino and surfing, this issue is jam packed with information.While the <strong><em>"Grim Truth of Fairy Tales"</em></strong> is very grim and quite horrific, the differences between folklore and fairy tales are explored in depth, playing into the Caps Curriculum for primary school grades.  Bearing in mind that the Brothers Grimm were the horror writers in the early 1800\u2019s, this is an article that may be better read during daylight hours or skipped entirely for younger, sensitive readers.A five page spread on <em>"The Religions of South Africa"</em> is very well written and presented, the addition of a world map displaying where each doctrine comes from.  Informative and enlightening, this is a good summary overall.<em>"What is Geocaching"</em>?  Found mainly in the vocabulary of technology browsers, this is the name given to the online Treasure Hunt game, suitable for the whole family.  Easy to understand with added tips and terms on how to play, <strong>Supernova</strong> includes a home based version with paper maps and tea bags to get your kids hunting at home.A great addition to the content is the careers section, in this instance covering the job of <em>"The textile designer"</em>, while the page on <em>"DJ Kino"</em> was well suited to youngsters too.  This is the story about fourteen year old Kino O\u2019Shea who was so desperate to be a DJ that his Dad called in to South Africa\u2019s most popular radio station, 94.7 Highveld Stereo.Surfing is the sport covered in this volume and is a four page spread of appealing pictures and facts.  We\u2019d like to suggest that a list of contacts for lessons be included at the end of articles like this, so that children who\u2019d like to try them can find out more.This edition\u2019s music page covers the <em>"Electronic Drums"</em> and together with <strong>Musica</strong>, offers kids the chance to rent any instrument for two weeks.  With no better way to see if your child has musical talent, parents can also introduce their children to entertainment skills with the article on <em>"Flash Mobs"</em>.  Becoming increasingly popular in South African shopping malls, children can discover how Flash Mobs started and developed.At times the <strong>Supernova</strong> pages can be very busy and become unclear, making the information difficult to process.  Colourful and fun, we have enjoyed reading it, but as the magazine is printed on high quality paper, this magazine is just too expensive for most single mums trying to make ends meet.  <strong>Supernova</strong> is however, a reusable item that we can come back to, again and again.Reviewed by Michelle, Jenna 12yrs and Connor 9yrs.                5/30/2014 5:28:22 AM\r                            5/30/2014 5:28:22 AM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Nikki McDiarmid reviews Oliver Strange and the Journey to the Swamps.\xa9 2016

<img alt="Boy running with frog" src="http://www.tafelberg.com/Books/Big/tafelberg_covers/childrens/2012/9780624054931.jpg"/><em>Oliver Strange</em> is the book that defines the right of passage from young readers to young adult books.Oliver is a boy unfamiliar with the realities of Africa, who must travel from London to Bulawayo, to the Victoria Falls, arriving eventually in Kasane.  It is here in Botswana, that Oliver\u2019s father has disappeared.In true local style, with relationships forged on the long journeys across the continent, Oliver meets the streetwise Zinzi.  Knowledgeable in conservation, Zinzi's school is part of Wild Care and Oliver is soon introduced to the bush-baby and rescued python that accompany her.  Zinzi speaks nine languages and knows every tree in Botswana.  Bright and as cute as a button, Oliver's new friend quickly has readers falling in love with her kind heart and quick wit.Before they know it, the two children are faced with danger. The criminals that lurk in the shadows cannot hide forever and Oliver notices a strange man who seems to be following them. Whispered conversations of a poisonous frog, with references to his herpetologist father, begin to lift the hairs on the back of his neck\u2026As an adult I am yet to get over the thrill in reading books that contain local content; hearing dialects that are familiar and nostalgic and recognising imagery so close to home. <em>Oliver Strange</em> is a feast for the mind, every sense is delighted by the colours of Africa, its people and its familiar aromas.<strong>Dianne Hofmeyr\u2019s</strong> writing is clear and precise, making for easy reading and imagery that will have children absolutely hooked.\u201c<em>Light bounced and dazzled and skittered</em>.\u201d<strong>Hofmeyr's</strong> descriptive phrases ensure that Oliver Strange both educates readers as well as transports them.As the tale unfolds, young readers are taught about animal compassion, while at the same time experiencing the \u2018<em>mission impossible</em>\u2019 danger linked so intrinsically to the life of conservationists.  Science and technology aspects of the story are juxtaposed against the cultural beliefs of poachers and \u2018muti-dealers\u2019, defining South Africa's diverse cultural ways.Not only educational, but terribly exciting too, <strong>Hofmeyr's</strong> cliff-hangers are SO exciting that even I battled to close the book at the end of every chapter. On the edge of my seat and holding my breath, I found each ending wonderfully, and often amusingly, resolved in the pages that followed.Reading <em>Oliver Strange and the Journey to the Swamp</em> was pure delight and is one journey guaranteed to keep kids enthralled. I certainly was!                6/18/2014 6:58:41 AM\r                            7/23/2014 10:42:27 AM\r

[<blockquote>Finally, after a long drought, here is an intelligent book aimed at  young South Africans in the eight-to-12 age group.  <br><br>   Vumile and the Dragon is well written and enhanced by exquisite  artwork in colour by botanical illustrator Lisa Strachan and in  halftones by Meg Jordi.  <br><br>   Katie, on a visit to her grandmother with her brother, Joe, follows  Granny Ada\u2019s black cat into a magical world of lush flowers and  foliage \u2026 and a baby dragon. Well, sort of.  <br><br>   The \u201cdragon\u201d turns out to be a chameleon. Katie and Joe are  fascinated, but their friend Vumile is less than impressed, because  Vumile\u2019s grandmother has passed on to her grandson many superstitions  about the strange little creatures with the independent eyes.  <br><br>   In the process of helping Vumile to overcome his fears, the three  acquire a considerable amount of information about the Cape dwarf  chameleon, its habits and its habitat.  <br><br>   The narrative should appeal to children who are fascinated by insects  and plants and to those who aren\u2019t quite so fascinated but like a good  story.  <br><br>  A section on \u201cother interesting facts about chameleons\u201d and an  illustrated guide to the plants and animals depicted in the detailed  colour illustrations add another learning element.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></blockquote>Source: http://mg.co.za/article/2013-03-28-00-its-all-about-the-right-words                5/2/2013 1:19:20 PM\r                            5/2/2013 1:19:20 PM\r

[<div class="span8"><p class="lead">Book Review 2: 'Thirteen' by Tom Hoyle\xa9 2016

<img alt="Boy silhouette on yellow" src="http://www.panmacmillan.com.au/cover1/9781447250449.jpg"/><strong>Sandy McCann reviews '<em>Thirteen</em>' by Tom Hoyle</strong>The book centres around Adam Grant, a 12 year old boy who is adopted and lives next door to Megan, his best friend whom he is developing feelings for.Coron, a man who as a child attacked his parents and was fixated with reading and studying books about the Devil, is the leader of \u201c<em>The People</em>\u201d cult. He and his followers, many of whom are brainwashed street children, believe that all boys born exactly on midnight at the start of the millennium (the year 2000) are evil and must be killed before the end of their thirteenth year to ensure that the Master's prophecies are fulfilled for the new Kingdom.It is, however, important that Adam is sacrificed spectacularly and a bombing in London is planned.The characters in the book were instantly believable.  Adam is your typical teenage boy coming of age.  Coron is a man whose state of mind is clearly not okay and he shows schizophrenic behaviours through his breakdown in the relation between his thoughts, emotions and behaviour.  He has withdrawn from reality and lives in a fantasy and delusional state.  His followers display characteristics of those that have been brainwashed into thinking he is the <em>'chosen one'</em> and that because they stand with him, no harm will come to them.In my field of work, I could relate to Coron his state of mind and with a teenage daughter of the same age, I could relate to what Adam was emotionally going through.  While having never joined a cult, I did, however, live with the Hare Krishna's for a week at the age of fifteen, only to find my mind changed very quickly once there.My least favourite part of <strong>Tom Hoyle's</strong> '<em>Thirteen</em>' was the idea of a 13 year old child killing someone. In the story, there is very little remorse from Adam after this act and in truth, this experience would be most devastating.  There was also no repercussion for his actions which I felt did not send a good message to young readers.The rest of the book was good reading and I enjoyed the friendships that Adam formed during his experience and how he put Megan\u2019s life before his own.I personally think the book is more suitable to kids of fifteen years and over as by this time they are fully aware of what's wrong and right and due to social media, are confronted with many cult-like issues, for example the fashion industry and the addiction to cell phones and drugs. Older teens can be more objective when reading this story.  <em>'Thirteen'</em> is an easy and well-written book that any teenager would enjoy reading as the plot moves fast, and, even though in some parts was predictable, the suspense still left you wanting to turn the pages.<img alt="Macmillan logo" src="http://geeksyndicate.co.uk/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/panmacmillan-140x140.jpg"/>                6/9/2014 9:56:52 AM\r                            6/9/2014 9:59:45 AM\r
